<meta name="DC.Description" content="Unusually, the single-celled eukaryote red alga, Galdieria sulphuraria , can thrive in hot, acidic springs. This organism is endowed with extraordinary metabolic talents and can consume a variety of strange carbohydrates, as well as turn on photosynthesis when the food runs out. Schönknecht et al. (p. [1207][1]; see the Perspective by Rocha ) discerned from phylogenetic analysis of its genome that during its evolution, G. sulphuraria appears to have commandeered at least 75 bacterial and archaeal genes by horizontal gene transfer and then applied gene expansion to boost its metabolic repertoire.

Some microbial eukaryotes, such as the extremophilic red alga Galdieria sulphuraria , live in hot, toxic metal-rich, acidic environments. To elucidate the underlying molecular mechanisms of adaptation, we sequenced the 13.7-megabase genome of G. sulphuraria . This alga shows an enormous metabolic flexibility, growing either photoautotrophically or heterotrophically on more than 50 carbon sources. Environmental adaptation seems to have been facilitated by horizontal gene transfer from various bacteria and archaea, often followed by gene family expansion. At least 5% of protein-coding genes of G. sulphuraria were probably acquired horizontally. These proteins are involved in ecologically important processes ranging from heavy-metal detoxification to glycerol uptake and metabolism. Thus, our findings show that a pan-domain gene pool has facilitated environmental adaptation in this unicellular eukaryote.

 [1]: /lookup/volpage/339/1207?iss=6124" />

Gagneul;citation_title=A reassessment of the function of the so-called compatible solutes in the halophytic plumbaginaceae Limonium latifolium;citation_volume=144;citation_year=2007;citation_pmid=17468212;citation_doi=10.1104/pp.107.099820" />
<meta name="twitter:title" content="Gene Transfer from Bacteria and Archaea Facilitated Evolution of an Extremophilic Eukaryote" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/339/6124/1207/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Unusually, the single-celled eukaryote red alga, Galdieria sulphuraria , can thrive in hot, acidic springs. This organism is endowed with extraordinary metabolic talents and can consume a variety of strange carbohydrates, as well as turn on photosynthesis when the food runs out. Schönknecht et al. (p. [1207][1]; see the Perspective by Rocha ) discerned from phylogenetic analysis of its genome that during its evolution, G. sulphuraria appears to have commandeered at least 75 bacterial and archaeal genes by horizontal gene transfer and then applied gene expansion to boost its metabolic repertoire.

Some microbial eukaryotes, such as the extremophilic red alga Galdieria sulphuraria , live in hot, toxic metal-rich, acidic environments. To elucidate the underlying molecular mechanisms of adaptation, we sequenced the 13.7-megabase genome of G. sulphuraria . This alga shows an enormous metabolic flexibility, growing either photoautotrophically or heterotrophically on more than 50 carbon sources. Environmental adaptation seems to have been facilitated by horizontal gene transfer from various bacteria and archaea, often followed by gene family expansion. At least 5% of protein-coding genes of G. sulphuraria were probably acquired horizontally. These proteins are involved in ecologically important processes ranging from heavy-metal detoxification to glycerol uptake and metabolism. Thus, our findings show that a pan-domain gene pool has facilitated environmental adaptation in this unicellular eukaryote.

 [1]: /lookup/volpage/339/1207?iss=6124" />

    <script id="loadcss">
      /*! CSS rel=preload polyfill. Depends on loadCSS function. [c]2016 @scottjehl, Filament Group, Inc. Licensed MIT  */
(function( w ){
  // rel=preload support test
  if( !w.loadCSS ){
    return;
  }
  var rp = loadCSS.relpreload = {};
  rp.support = function(){
    try {
      return w.document.createElement( "link" ).relList.supports( "preload" );
    } catch (e) {
      return false;
    }
  };

  // loop preload links and fetch using loadCSS
  rp.poly = function(){
    var links = w.document.getElementsByTagName( "link" );
    for( var i = 0; i < links.length; i++ ){
      var link = links[ i ];
      if( link.rel === "preload" && link.getAttribute( "as" ) === "style" ){
        w.loadCSS( link.href, link );
        link.rel = null;
      }
    }
  };

  // if link[rel=preload] is not supported, we must fetch the CSS manually using loadCSS
  if( !rp.support() ){
    rp.poly();
    var run = w.setInterval( rp.poly, 300 );
    if( w.addEventListener ){
      w.addEventListener( "load", function(){
        w.clearInterval( run );
      } );
    }
    if( w.attachEvent ){
      w.attachEvent( "onload", function(){
        w.clearInterval( run );
      } )
    }
  }
}( this ));

<div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container article__body">
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_sci_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_sci_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup">
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" data-highwire-glossary-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="section editor-summary" id="abstract-1"><h2 class="">Hot, Toxic Eukaryote</h2><p id="p-5">Unusually, the single-celled eukaryote red alga, <em>Galdieria sulphuraria</em>, can thrive in hot, acidic springs. This organism is endowed with extraordinary metabolic talents and can consume a variety of strange carbohydrates, as well as turn on photosynthesis when the food runs out. <strong>Schönknecht <em>et al.</em></strong> (p. <a href="/lookup/volpage/339/1207?iss=6124">1207</a>; see the Perspective by <strong>Rocha</strong>) discerned from phylogenetic analysis of its genome that during its evolution, <em>G. sulphuraria</em> appears to have commandeered at least 75 bacterial and archaeal genes by horizontal gene transfer and then applied gene expansion to boost its metabolic repertoire.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-3"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-7">Some microbial eukaryotes, such as the extremophilic red alga <em>Galdieria sulphuraria</em>, live in hot, toxic metal-rich, acidic environments. To elucidate the underlying molecular mechanisms of adaptation, we sequenced the 13.7-megabase genome of <em>G. sulphuraria</em>. This alga shows an enormous metabolic flexibility, growing either photoautotrophically or heterotrophically on more than 50 carbon sources. Environmental adaptation seems to have been facilitated by horizontal gene transfer from various bacteria and archaea, often followed by gene family expansion. At least 5% of protein-coding genes of <em>G. sulphuraria</em> were probably acquired horizontally. These proteins are involved in ecologically important processes ranging from heavy-metal detoxification to glycerol uptake and metabolism. Thus, our findings show that a pan-domain gene pool has facilitated environmental adaptation in this unicellular eukaryote.</p></div><p id="p-8">Although bacteria and archaea usually dominate extreme environments, hot and extremely acidic habitats are typically devoid of photosynthetic bacteria. Instead, eukaryotic unicellular red algae of the Cyanidiophyceae are the principal photosynthetic organisms in these ecological niches (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1"><em>1</em></a>). Cyanidiophyceae can grow at pH 0 to 4 and temperatures up to 56°C, close to the upper temperature limit for eukaryotic life (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2"><em>2</em></a>). <em>Galdieria sulphuraria</em> is a unique member of the Cyanidiophyceae, displaying high salt and metal tolerance and exhibiting extensive metabolic versatility (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3"><em>3</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4"><em>4</em></a>). <em>G. sulphuraria</em> naturally inhabits volcanic hot sulfur springs, solfatara soils, and anthropogenic hostile environments. In habitats with high concentrations of arsenic, aluminum, cadmium, mercury, and other toxic metals, <em>G. sulphuraria</em> frequently represents up to 90% of total biomass and almost all the eukaryotic biomass (<a id="xref-ref-1-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1"><em>1</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>).</p><p id="p-9">To understand the molecular mechanisms underlying <em>G. sulphuraria</em>'s extremophilic and metabolically flexible lifestyle (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>), we determined its genome sequence (13.7 Mb; table S1) (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6"><em>6</em></a>). The only member of the Cyanidiophyceae for which a genome sequence was previously available, <em>Cyanidioschyzon merolae</em> (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>), diverged from <em>G. sulphuraria</em> about 1 billion years ago, which approximates the evolutionary distance between fruit flies and humans (see fig. S1 and supplementary materials). <em>C. merolae</em> maintains a strictly photoautotrophic lifestyle and does not tolerate high salt or metal concentrations; it differs markedly from <em>G. sulphuraria</em> in ecology, cell biology, and physiology. Accordingly, we find orthologs for only 42% of the 6623 <em>G. sulphuraria</em> proteins in <em>C. merolae</em>, and only 25% of both genomes constitute syntenic blocks (fig. S2). Coding sequences make up 77.5% of the <em>G. sulphuraria</em> genome, resulting in a median intergenic distance of 20 base pairs (bp) (fig. S3). Protein-coding genes contain on average two introns (fig. S4), with median lengths of 55 bp (fig. S5). Thus, the <em>G. sulphuraria</em> genome is highly condensed by comparison with that of <em>C. merolae</em> and most other eukaryotes.</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F1">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Photoautotrophic (left) and heterotrophic (right) G. sulphuraria cells. Cell cultures (top) and light microscopic images (bottom; bar represents 10 &#x3BC;m) of G. sulphuraria cells grown under continuous illumination in the absence of glucose (left) or in darkness in the presence of 200 mM glucose (right)." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1890193857" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 1&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-10&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;Photoautotrophic (left) and heterotrophic (right) &lt;em&gt;G. sulphuraria&lt;/em&gt; cells. Cell cultures (top) and light microscopic images (bottom; bar represents 10 &#x3BC;m) of &lt;em&gt;G. sulphuraria&lt;/em&gt; cells grown under continuous illumination in the absence of glucose (left) or in darkness in the presence of 200 mM glucose (right).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F1-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F1.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F1-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F1.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 1"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/564035" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F1-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 1</span> <p id="p-10" class="first-child">Photoautotrophic (left) and heterotrophic (right) <em>G. sulphuraria</em> cells. Cell cultures (top) and light microscopic images (bottom; bar represents 10 μm) of <em>G. sulphuraria</em> cells grown under continuous illumination in the absence of glucose (left) or in darkness in the presence of 200 mM glucose (right).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-11">Eukaryotic innovations usually arise through gene duplications and neofunctionalizations, which lead to expansion of existing gene families (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8"><em>8</em></a>). In contrast, archaea and bacteria commonly adapt through horizontal gene transfer (HGT) from other lineages (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9"><em>9</em></a>). HGT has also been observed in some unicellular eukaryotes (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10"><em>10</em></a>); however, to our knowledge, horizontally acquired genes have not been linked to fitness-relevant traits in free-living eukaryotes (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11"><em>11</em></a>). Phylogenetic analyses of <em>G. sulphuraria</em> genes using highly stringent criteria indicate at least 75 separate gene acquisitions from archaea and bacteria (supplementary materials). The origin of these <em>G. sulphuraria</em> genes from HGT is supported by the finding that compared to the genomic average, they have significantly fewer introns (mean 0.8 versus 2.06, <em>P</em> = 0.0012, Mann-Whitney test; fig. S6), slightly higher GC content (40.6% versus 39.9%, <em>P</em> = 0.0030, Student's <em>t</em> test; fig. S7), and deviating oligonucleotide usage (<em>P</em> = 0.00034, Mann-Whitney test; fig. S7). Gene transfers can be traced to a broad range of donor taxa (fig. S8 and table S4), with a significant enrichment from extremophile bacteria (<em>P</em> = 7.8 × 10<sup>−9</sup>, Fisher's exact test). The genome of <em>G. sulphuraria</em> thus shows notable contributions from a pan-domain gene pool.</p><p id="p-12">The two largest <em>G. sulphuraria</em> protein families form a monophyletic branch within the so-called archaeal ATPases (adenosine triphosphatases) (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2A</a>). These soluble ATPases have not been observed in other eukaryotes. Phylogenetic analyses indicate that <em>G. sulphuraria</em> acquired an ancestral ATPase gene from archaea, followed by duplications and diversification into separate families (fig. S11). Genes encoding the different families cluster in pairs on the <em>G. sulphuraria</em> genome (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2B</a>). Pairs of homologous ATPase genes that are transcribed together are observed in archaeal genomes (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12"><em>12</em></a>). Although their physiological function is unknown (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13"><em>13</em></a>), archaeal ATPases may contribute to heat tolerance. We found a correlation between ATPase gene copy number and optimal growth temperature across thermophilic and hyperthermophilic archaea (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2C</a>). These findings suggest that <em>G. sulphuraria</em>'s adaptation to heat may have been facilitated by the acquisition and subsequent expansion of an archaeal ATPase gene family.</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F2">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Archaeal ATPases in G. sulphuraria. (A) The phylogeny of archaeal ATPases indicates HGT into G. sulphuraria. The unrooted Bayesian tree is shown with posterior probabilities (full tree and details in fig. (C) Copy number of archaeal ATPase genes in genomes of thermophilic and hyperthermophilic archaea is correlated with optimum growth temperature (for details, see fig. S12)." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1890193857" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 2&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-13&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;Archaeal ATPases in &lt;em&gt;G. sulphuraria&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;strong&gt;C&lt;/strong&gt;) Copy number of archaeal ATPase genes in genomes of thermophilic and hyperthermophilic archaea is correlated with optimum growth temperature (for details, see fig. S12).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F2-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F2.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F2-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F2.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 2"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/564043" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F2-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 2</span> <p id="p-13" class="first-child">Archaeal ATPases in <em>G. sulphuraria</em>. (<strong>A</strong>) The phylogeny of archaeal ATPases indicates HGT into <em>G. sulphuraria</em>. The unrooted Bayesian tree is shown with posterior probabilities (full tree and details in fig. S9, alignment of ATPase domains in fig. S10, and detailed tree of families #1a, #1b, #2, and #19 in fig. S11). Major phylogenetic groups are color-coded (Archaea, gray; Cyanobacteria, teal; other Bacteria, olive; Rhodophyta, red; Amoebozoa, purple) and labeled according to Leipe <em>et al</em>. (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20"><em>20</em></a>). (<strong>B</strong>) Genes encoding archaeal ATPases in <em>G. sulphuraria</em> form unidirectional clusters in specific combinations; pairs are always formed from different (sub)families, with #1b genes always at the 5′ end. (<strong>C</strong>) Copy number of archaeal ATPase genes in genomes of thermophilic and hyperthermophilic archaea is correlated with optimum growth temperature (for details, see fig. S12).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-14"><em>G. sulphuraria</em>'s tolerance to high salinity is also likely to have been facilitated by HGT. Resistance to salt stress requires the removal of Na<sup>+</sup> from the cytosol and an increase of osmolarity using compatible solutes in the cytosol. In addition to several Na<sup>+</sup>:H<sup>+</sup> antiporters of eukaryotic origin, <em>G. sulphuraria</em> encodes two monovalent cation:proton antiporters that appear to have been acquired from bacteria (fig. S13). Furthermore, genes encoding sarcosine dimethylglycine methyltransferase (SDMT) appear to originate from halophilic cyanobacteria (fig. S14). These enzymes allow the production of the compatible solute betaine from glycine (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14"><em>14</em></a>), which indeed accumulates in <em>G. sulphuraria</em> under salt stress (fig. S15).</p><p id="p-15">How does <em>G. sulphuraria</em> maintain near-neutral cytosolic pH against a 10<sup>6</sup>-fold H<sup>+</sup> gradient across its plasma membrane (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15"><em>15</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16"><em>16</em></a>)? There is no evidence for an enhanced capacity to pump H<sup>+</sup> out of the cytosol (which would be an energetically intense strategy). Yet, there are indications of a reduced H<sup>+</sup> permeability of the plasma membrane. For <em>G. sulphuraria</em>, one voltage-gated ion channel gene was identified in the genome, compared to three in <em>C. merolae</em> (table S3) and 16 or more in other unicellular algae. Voltage-gated ion channels allow single-file diffusion of water, and therefore have a very high conductance for protons. A plasma membrane devoid of proton-conducting voltage-gated ion channels probably has a low H<sup>+</sup> permeability, preventing the acidification of the cell interior even at very high external H<sup>+</sup> concentrations. <em>G. sulphuraria</em> shows an expansion of both, the intracellular chloride channel (CLIC) family and the chloride carrier/channel (ClC) family (table S3), which do not conduct protons.</p><p id="p-16"><em>G. sulphuraria</em> copes with toxic metals typically found in volcanic areas and acid mine drainage by a variety of metal transporters (table S3). Different plasma membrane uptake systems for divalent metal cations permit the selective uptake of essential metals (such as iron or copper) at high concentrations of toxic metals (such as aluminum or cadmium). <em>G. sulphuraria</em> can also neutralize biohazardous metals, making it potentially useful in biotechnological applications. For example, <em>G. sulphuraria</em> arsenite methyltransferases can biotransform arsenic, which is often found at very high concentrations in geothermal environments, into less toxic and possibly gaseous methyl derivatives (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). In addition, two intronless <em>G. sulphuraria</em> genes encode the bacterial arsenical membrane protein pump, ArsB. The sequences most similar to <em>G. sulphuraria</em> ArsB are from thermoacidophilic bacteria (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>), again indicating a central role for HGT in extremophilic adaptation. Mercury is found at concentrations up to 200 μg/g in soils from which <em>G. sulphuraria</em> has been isolated. <em>G. sulphuraria</em> can reduce cytotoxic Hg<sup>2+</sup> into less toxic metallic mercury. The enzyme responsible, mercuric reductase, was also most likely acquired horizontally from Proteobacteria (fig. S17).</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F3">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="The phylogeny (Bayesian tree) of bacterial arsenical resistance efflux pumps (ArsB) indicates HGT into G. sulphuraria (full tree and details in fig. S16). The curly bracket marks G. sulphuraria and four thermophilic and/or acidophilic bacteria (in olive), which live in the same environment as G. sulphuraria and from which taxa the algal ArsB may have derived." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1890193857" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 3&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-17&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;The phylogeny (Bayesian tree) of bacterial arsenical resistance efflux pumps (ArsB) indicates HGT into &lt;em&gt;G. sulphuraria&lt;/em&gt; (full tree and details in fig. S16). The curly bracket marks &lt;em&gt;G. sulphuraria&lt;/em&gt; and four thermophilic and/or acidophilic bacteria (in olive), which live in the same environment as &lt;em&gt;G. sulphuraria&lt;/em&gt; and from which taxa the algal ArsB may have derived.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F3-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F3.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F3-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F3.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 3"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/339/6124/1207/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/564051" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F3-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 3</span> <p id="p-17" class="first-child">The phylogeny (Bayesian tree) of bacterial arsenical resistance efflux pumps (ArsB) indicates HGT into <em>G. sulphuraria</em> (full tree and details in fig. S16). The curly bracket marks <em>G. sulphuraria</em> and four thermophilic and/or acidophilic bacteria (in olive), which live in the same environment as <em>G. sulphuraria</em> and from which taxa the algal ArsB may have derived.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-18">In total, 5.2% of <em>G. sulphuraria</em> genes encode membrane transport proteins (mostly metabolite transporters), which is more than has been discovered in most other eukaryotes (fig. S18). Gene family expansions (table S3) are found, for example, in sugar porters (fig. S19), amino acid/auxin permeases (fig. S20), and putative acetate transporters (fig. S21); these three protein families are among the 20 largest in <em>G. sulphuraria</em> (table S2). Further major expansions were found in amino acid–polyamine–organocation transporters (fig. S22), glycoside-pentoside-hexuronide:cation symporters, and glycerol uptake facilitators (fig. S23). Together, these metabolite transporters are likely to be essential for the exceptional ability of <em>G. sulphuraria</em> to grow heterotrophically on many different metabolites. In contrast, most other unicellular algae, including <em>C. merolae</em>, are strictly (or almost strictly) photoautotrophic.</p><p id="p-19">Again, phylogenetic analyses indicate that HGT contributed to <em>G. sulphuraria</em>'s enormous metabolic flexibility. All acetate permeases (fig. S21) appear to originate from bacteria, whereas some of the amino acid–polyamine–organocation transporters (fig. S22) seem to stem from thermoacidophilic archaea. <em>G. sulphuraria</em> can grow heterotrophically on glycerol as sole carbon source (<a id="xref-ref-3-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3"><em>3</em></a>) using a family of five glycerol uptake facilitators and a family of three glycerol dehydrogenases; both families apparently originate from HGT (figs. S23 and S24).</p><p id="p-20">Some of <em>G. sulphuraria</em>'s metabolic pathways appear to be conserved from a heterotrophic last common eukaryotic ancestor, but subsequently were lost from other photosynthetic eukaryotes. For example, animals (metazoa) use the methylmalonyl–coenzyme A pathway for the degradation of odd-numbered chain fatty acids and leucine. Whereas green plants and <em>C. merolae</em> lack this pathway, it is present in <em>G. sulphuraria</em>, as well as in diatoms and brown algae. Furthermore, green plants, diatoms, and brown algae synthesize the essential nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide precursor quinolinate from aspartate. In contrast, <em>G. sulphuraria</em> and <em>C. merolae</em> produce quinolinate from tryptophan by way of kynurenine, a pathway common to animals and fungi.</p><p id="p-21"><em>G. sulphuraria</em> contains a number of metabolite transporter families that group with fungi and show less similarity to metabolite transporter families from more closely related organisms (figs. S19, S20, and S25). This unexpected phylogenetic trace could be explained by the loss of genes present in the common eukaryotic ancestor from other clades, by HGT from unsequenced bacteria or archaea into both eukaryotic lineages, or by HGT between fungi and <em>G. sulphuraria</em>. We currently cannot distinguish between these alternative hypotheses owing to limited taxon representation in sequence databases.</p><p id="p-22"><em>G. sulphuraria</em> can also survive saprophytically by excreting catabolic enzymes that decompose extracellular organic polymers into small metabolites for uptake by plasma membrane transporters. Proteomics (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18"><em>18</em></a>) and/or bioinformatics analyses (supplementary materials) indicate excretion of aspartyl proteases, which cleave peptide bonds at acidic pH; β-galactosidases (fig. S26) and glucoamylases, which degrade polysaccharides; and acid phosphatases (fig. S27), which remove phosphate groups from organic molecules. These excreted enzymes lack orthologs in other photosynthetic eukaryotes, but show homology to excreted enzymes encoded by fungi or bacteria. In particular, <em>G. sulphuraria</em> may have acquired acid phosphatases (fig. S27) and some β-galactosidases (fig. S26) from bacteria.</p><p id="p-23">Extensive gene transfer appears to have been key to the genomic evolution of a metabolically versatile, extremophilic, red alga. Numerous proteins acquired through HGT interact with <em>G. sulphuraria</em>'s physico-chemical and metabolic environment. Protein families acquired horizontally by <em>G. sulphuraria</em> are 3-fold enriched in membrane transporters (10.5% for HGT families, <em>P</em> = 0.010, Fisher's exact test) and 14-fold enriched in protein families also found in extremophilic bacteria or archaea (86.8% for HGT families, <em>P</em> = 1.5 × 10<sup>−22</sup>). These findings for <em>G. sulphuraria</em> mirror the results of a previous systematic study, which showed that proteobacterial adaptation relies on the horizontal acquisition of genes that function at the bacteria's interface to the environment (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19"><em>19</em></a>). Whereas the importance of HGT for evolution of Bacteria and Archaea is well established, adaptation of a eukaryotic extremophile by gene transfer from Bacteria and Archaea is unexpected and shines a new light on the evolution of unicellular eukaryotes.</p><div class="section app" id="app-1"><h2 class="">Supplementary Materials</h2><div id="DC1" class="supplementary-material"><p id="p-24"><a href="http://www.sciencemag.org/cgi/content/full/339/6124/1207/DC1">www.sciencemag.org/cgi/content/full/339/6124/1207/DC1</a></p><p id="p-25">Materials and Methods</p><p id="p-26">Supplementary Text</p><p id="p-27">Figs. S1 to S27</p><p id="p-28">Tables S1 to S3</p><p id="p-29">Additional Data Table S4</p><p id="p-30">References (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21"><em>21</em></a><em>–</em><a id="xref-ref-79-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-79"><em>79</em></a>)</p></div></div><div class="section ref-list" id="ref-list-1"><h2 class="">References and Notes</h2><ol class="cit-list"><li><a class="rev-xref-ref" href="#xref-ref-1-1" title="View reference 1 in text" id="ref-1">↵</a><div class="cit ref-cit ref-journal" id="cit-339.6124.1207.1" data-doi="10.1099/00221287-67-1-17"><div class="cit-metadata"><ol class="cit-auth-list"><li><span class="cit-auth"> <span class="cit-name-given-names">W. <span class="cit-fpage">1598</span> (<span class="cit-pub-date">2007</span>).<span class="cit-pub-id-sep cit-pub-id-doi-sep"> </span><span class="cit-pub-id cit-pub-id-doi"><span class="cit-pub-id-scheme-doi">doi:</span>10.1104/pp.107.099820</span><span class="cit-pub-id-sep cit-pub-id-pmid-sep"> </span><span class="cit-pub-id cit-pub-id-pmid"><span class="cit-pub-id-scheme-pmid">pmid:</span>17468212</span></cite></div><div class="cit-extra"><a href="{openurl}?query=rft.jtitle%253DPlant%2BPhysiol.%26rft_id%253Dinfo%253Adoi%252F10.1104%252Fpp.107.099820%26rft_id%253Dinfo%253Apmid%252F17468212%26rft.genre%253Darticle%26rft_val_fmt%253Dinfo%253Aofi%252Ffmt%253Akev%253Amtx%253Ajournal%26ctx_ver%253DZ39.88-2004%26url_ver%253DZ39.88-2004%26url_ctx_fmt%253Dinfo%253Aofi%252Ffmt%253Akev%253Amtx%253Actx" class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-openurl cit-ref-sprinkles-open-url"><span>OpenUrl</span></a><a href="/lookup/ijlink/YTozOntzOjQ6InBhdGgiO3M6MTQ6Ii9sb29rdXAvaWpsaW5rIjtzOjU6InF1ZXJ5IjthOjQ6e3M6ODoibGlua1R5cGUiO3M6NDoiQUJTVCI7czoxMToiam91cm5hbENvZGUiO3M6MTI6InBsYW50cGh5c2lvbCI7czo1OiJyZXNpZCI7czoxMDoiMTQ0LzMvMTU5OCI7czo0OiJhdG9tIjtzOjIzOiIvc2NpLzMzOS82MTI0LzEyMDcuYXRvbSI7fXM6ODoiZnJhZ21lbnQiO3M6MDoiIjt9" class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-ijlink"><span><span class="cit-reflinks-abstract">Abstract</span><span class="cit-sep cit-reflinks-variant-name-sep">/</span><span class="cit-reflinks-full-text"><span class="free-full-text">FREE </span>Full Text</span></span></a></div></div></li><li><div class="cit ref-cit ack no-rev-xref"><strong>Acknowledgments: </strong>This work was made possible by NSF grant EF 0332882 (to A.P.M.W.).             <p  class="highwire-cite-authors byline">By <span  class="highwire-citation-authors"><span class="highwire-citation-author first" data-delta="0">Gerald Schönknecht</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="1">Wei-Hua Chen</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="2">Chad M. Ternes</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="3">Guillaume G. Barbier</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="4">Roshan P. Shrestha</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="5">Mario Stanke</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="6">Andrea Bräutigam</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="7">Brett J. Baker</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="8">Jillian F. Banfield</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="9">R. Michael Garavito</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="10">Kevin Carr</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="11">Curtis Wilkerson</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="12">Stefan A. Rensing</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="13">David Gagneul</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="14">Nicholas E. Dickenson</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="15">Christine Oesterhelt</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="16">Martin J. Lercher</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="17">Andreas P. M. Weber</span></span></p>
            <p  class="highwire-cite-metadata byline"><em>Science</em><time>08 Mar 2013</time> : 1207-1210 </p>
                <div class="highwire-cite-snippet media__deck">
      <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="section precis" id="abstract-2" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><p id="p-6">A mosaic of genes acquired from various phyla enable a red alga to grow abundantly in hot, acidic, and toxic niches. <span class="named-content related-content" id="named-content-1">[Also see Perspective by <a href="/lookup/volpage/339/1154?iss=6124">Rocha</a>]</span></p></div></div>    </div>
        <ul class="variant-list media__links"><li class="supplement-data first last"><a href="/content/339/6124/1207/suppl/DC1" class="highwire-variant-link variant-supplemental-material" title="Supplementary Materials" rel="alternate" type="text/html" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Supplementary Materials</a></li>
</ul>  </div>
</article></div>  </div>
